;; ========================================

let person := {:name "Alice"
               :age 42
               :active true}
let name := [m/get person :name]
let older := [m/update person :age inc]

;; ========================================
;; Tuple (()) - if you want them
;; ========================================

let pair : (Nat, String) := (42, "answer")
let (n, s) := pair    ;; destructuring
#+end_src

---

- Comparison Table

| Collection | Sigil | Persistent? | Lazy? | Indexed? | Example     |
|------------+-------+-------------+-------+----------+-------------|
| Grouping   | =[]=  | N/A         | N/A   | N/A      | =[add 1 2]= |
| Vector     | =@[]= | ✅          | ❌    | ✅ O(1)  | =@[1 2 3]=  |
| List       | ='[]= | ✅          | ❌    | ❌ O(n)  | ='[1 2 3]=  |
| Lazy Seq   | =~[]= | ✅          | ✅    | ❌       | =~[1 2 3]=  |
| Set        | =#{}= | ✅          | ❌    | ❌       | =#{1 2 3}=  |
| Map        | ={}=  | ✅          | ❌    | ✅ O(1)  | ={:a 1}=    |
| Tuple      | =()=  | ✅          | ❌    | ✅ O(1)  | =(1, "a")=  |

---

- Alternative Sigils (If You Don't Like These)

| Collection | Alt 1   | Alt 2   | Alt 3    |
|------------+---------+---------+----------|
| Vector     | =@[]=   | =v[]=   | =#v[]=   |
| List       | ='[]=   | =l[]=   | =#l[]=   |
| Lazy Seq   | =~[]=   | =s[]=   | =#~[]=   |
| Set        | =#{}=   | =s{}=   | =#s{}=   |
| Map        | ={}=    | =m{}=   | =#m{}=   |

The single-character sigils (=@=, ='=, =~=) are most ergonomic.

---

- Handling TCL Inspiration

TCL uses =[]= for command substitution:

#+begin_src tcl
set x [expr 1 + 2]   ;; [] = evaluate command
set y {1 2 3}        ;; {} = literal list (no evaluation)
#+end_src

You could adopt similar philosophy:

#+begin_src prologos
;; [] = evaluate/group (like TCL)
[add 1 2]

;; Sigiled brackets = data literals (no evaluation)
@[1 2 3]     ;; vector data
'[1 2 3]     ;; list data
~[1 2 3]     ;; seq data
#+end_src

This is actually very clean: =[]= is always "code", sigiled brackets are always "data."

---

- Lazy Seq Alternatives

For lazy sequences specifically:

| Syntax        | Pros                     | Cons                    |
|---------------+--------------------------+-------------------------|
| =~[...]=      | Short, wave = lazy       | =~= less familiar       |
| =&[...]=      | =&= = generator/lazy     | =&= used elsewhere      |
| =$[...]=      | Different                | =$ = money?             |
| =*[...]=      | Star = stream            | =*= = multiplication    |
| =seq[...]=    | Explicit                 | Verbose                 |
| =#~[...]=     | Consistent with =#{}=    | Two characters          |

I still prefer =~[...]= for its visual lightness and "wavy" suggestion of laziness.

---

- Final Recommendation

#+begin_src prologos
;; Grouping (code)
[...]

;; Vector (persistent, indexed)
@[1 2 3]

;; List (persistent, sequential)
'[1 2 3]

;; Lazy Seq (lazy, sequential)
~[1 2 3]

;; Set (persistent, unordered)
#{1 2 3}

;; Map (persistent, key-value)
{:a 1, :b 2}

;; Tuple (fixed-size, typed)
(1, "a", true)
#+end_src

This gives you:
- ✅ Clean =[]= for grouping
- ✅ Distinct sigils for each collection type
- ✅ Ergonomic typing (single character sigils)
- ✅ Mnemonic (=@= = at/index, ='= = literal, =~= = lazy)
- ✅ EDN-compatible sets and maps
- ✅ No context-dependence (homoiconicity preserved)
- ✅ TCL-inspired philosophy (=[]= = code, sigiled = data)

---

Does this feel right? Would you like to explore any of these sigils further?


* I agree with your proposed sigils--nice find on the Seq with the `~[..]`. A good rationale. One thing we have currently supported in the prototype is anonymous functions and `defn` use `[]` for argument lists. How much does this pollute our homoiconicity/code-is-data? I mean, if it's macro-expanded are we in a clean spot? What considerations should we have in mind on this point?

- Argument Lists and Homoiconicity

Great question. Let me analyze this carefully.

---

- Current Syntax

#+begin_src prologos
;; Function definition
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; Anonymous function
fn [x : Nat] [add x 1]

;; But [] is also grouping
[add 1 2]
#+end_src

The same =[]= means:
1. Argument list (in =defn=, =fn=)
2. Grouping/application (in body)

---

- Is This a Problem for Homoiconicity?

/Short answer: Not really, if you're consistent about the AST./

Let me explain:

- What Homoiconicity Actually Requires

Homoiconicity means: /code has a direct, predictable AST representation/.

#+begin_src prologos
;; Source
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; AST (one possible representation)
[defn add
  [params [x : Nat] [y : Nat]]
  [return-type Nat]
  [body [plus x y]]]

;; Or simpler
[defn add [[x Nat] [y Nat]] Nat
  [plus x y]]
#+end_src

The key: the /parser produces the same AST structure every time/. The =[]= in argument position is recognized by its location within the =defn= form, not by its contents.

- Comparison: How Clojure Handles This

#+begin_src clojure
;; Clojure source
(defn add [x y]
  (+ x y))

;; AST
(defn add [x y]    ;; [x y] is a vector literal!
  (+ x y))         ;; (+ x y) is a list

;; The vector [x y] is literally a vector value in the AST
;; defn macro interprets it as parameter list
#+end_src

Clojure's =[]= is /always/ a vector. The =defn= macro /interprets/ that vector as parameters. This is perfectly homoiconic.

---

- Your Situation: Two Options

- Option A: =[]= Is Always Grouping (Parser-Level)

#+begin_src prologos
;; Source
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; AST ([] becomes a grouping node everywhere)
[defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]]

;; Parser produces:
{form: 'defn
 name: 'add
 params: {form: 'group, children: [{name: 'x, type: 'Nat}, {name: 'y, type: 'Nat}]}
 return: 'Nat
 body: {form: 'group, children: ['plus, 'x, 'y]}}
#+end_src

/Verdict:/ The =[]= is always "group" at AST level. Its /semantic meaning/ (params vs call) comes from position within the parent form.

This is fine! It's how Lisp works: =()= is always "list", meaning comes from position.

- Option B: =[]= Gets Different AST Based on Context

#+begin_src prologos
;; BAD: Same syntax, different AST nodes
[x : Nat, y : Nat]  ;; → ParamList node
[plus x y]          ;; → Application node
#+end_src

This would break homoiconicity. Avoid this.

---

- The Clean Design

/Make =[]= always produce the same AST node type./

#+begin_src prologos
;; Source
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; AST (uniform)
(defn add
  (bracket (x : Nat) (y : Nat))  ;; bracket node
  Nat                             ;; return type
  (bracket plus x y))             ;; bracket node
#+end_src

Both =[]=s are "bracket" nodes. The =defn= macro/compiler knows:
- First bracket after name = params
- Bracket in body position = application

---

- Macro Expansion: Where Meaning Is Assigned

#+begin_src prologos
;; defn is a macro that expands:
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; Expands to something like:
def add
  fn [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
    [plus x y]

;; fn is also a macro:
fn [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]

;; Expands to core lambda:
(lambda ((x Nat) (y Nat)) Nat
  (apply plus x y))
#+end_src

At each stage, the macro interprets the bracket's meaning based on its position.

---

- What You Should Ensure

- 1. Parser Produces Uniform AST

#+begin_src prologos
;; These all produce the same node type
[a b c]        ;; → Bracket([a, b, c])
[x : Nat]      ;; → Bracket([Typed(x, Nat)])
[]             ;; → Bracket([])
#+end_src

- 2. Semantic Interpretation Happens Later

#+begin_src prologos
;; defn macro sees:
(defn add Bracket([Typed(x,Nat), Typed(y,Nat)]) Nat Bracket([plus,x,y]))

;; It interprets:
;;   - second element (Bracket) as params
;;   - third element as return type
;;   - fourth element (Bracket) as body (application)
#+end_src

- 3. Quote Preserves Structure

#+begin_src prologos
;; Quoting captures the bracket node
$[add 1 2]
;; => Bracket(['add, 1, 2])

;; Not
;; => Application('add, [1, 2])  ;; this would break homoiconicity
#+end_src

---

- Practical Test: Can You Manipulate Code As Data?

#+begin_src prologos
;; If homoiconic, this should work:

;; Get the params from a function definition
let my-defn $[defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat [plus x y]]

[second my-defn]           ;; => 'add
[third my-defn]            ;; => $[x : Nat, y : Nat]  (a bracket node)
[fourth my-defn]           ;; => 'Nat
[fifth my-defn]            ;; => $[plus x y]  (a bracket node)

;; Transform it
:= new-defn
  [list 'defn
        'subtract
        [third my-defn]     ;; reuse params
        [fourth my-defn]    ;; reuse return type
        $[minus x y]]       ;; new body

;; new-defn is now:
;; $[defn subtract [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat [minus x y]]
#+end_src

If this works, you're homoiconic. The =[]= is just a container; meaning comes from position and interpretation.

---

- Considerations for Your Implementation

| Concern           | Question                            | Recommendation                  |
|-------------------+-------------------------------------+---------------------------------|
| AST uniformity    | Does =[]= always produce same node? | Yes, always =Bracket=           |
| Parser simplicity | Does parser need context?           | No, just produce =Bracket=      |
| Macro hygiene     | Can macros see raw brackets?        | Yes, before interpretation      |
| Quote behavior    | Does =$[...]= preserve structure?   | Yes, returns =Bracket=          |
| Pretty printing   | Can you round-trip code?            | Yes, if AST → syntax is uniform |

---

- Potential Syntax Ambiguity

One thing to watch:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Is this a function call or something else?
[f [a b]]

;; As call: f applied to (a b)
;; As nested: bracket containing f and bracket containing a b

;; With your syntax, it's:
[f [a b]]  ;; call f with argument [a b]
           ;; where [a b] is... a call to a with b? or a vector?
#+end_src

/This is where sigils help:/

#+begin_src prologos
[f @[a b]]  ;; call f with vector [a b]
[f [a b]]   ;; call f with result of (a b)
[f '[a b]]  ;; call f with list [a b]
#+end_src

Clear and unambiguous!

---

- Summary

| Question                                  | Answer                      |
|-------------------------------------------+-----------------------------|
| Does =[]= for params break homoiconicity? | No, if AST is uniform       |
| What should =[]= produce?                 | Always a =Bracket= node     |
| Where does meaning come from?             | Position within parent form |
| When is it interpreted?                   | Macro expansion time        |
| Is macro expansion enough?                | Yes, this is how Lisp works |

/Your approach is fine./ Just ensure:

1. =[]= always produces =Bracket= at parse time
2. Macros interpret based on position
3. Quote captures raw =Bracket= structure
4. Sigils distinguish data literals from grouping

You're in a clean spot!











* Currently we have `let` as a let form only

#+begin_src prologos

  let [x : Nat 4   ;; we don't need types here, right?
       y : Nat 3]
    nat/add x y

  let [xs : List Nat '[1 2 3]]  ;; will this parse? Not sure
    xs

  let [xs : [List Nat] '[1 2 3]] ;; would this be better? more clear/clean?

  ;; I'm favoring this syntax:

  let x : Nat := 42  ;; with the `:=` I think this symbol should be optional; is that possible?

#+end_src

I haven't tested out for myself quite yet, if let can even properly parse a multi-part type without angle brackets such as `x : List Nat _`

Can we define `let` with both a binding form style, and still use the keyword for inline-style?  What are your design notes/critique of these considerations? What do you think will play well for most people, drawing from the lessons of all other languages?

- Designing =let= Syntax

This is a meaty design question. Let me work through it systematically.

---

- The Core Tension

You want:
1. Block-style let (multiple bindings)
2. Inline-style let (single binding, flows into expression)
3. Optional type annotations
4. Optional =:==
5. Multi-part types like =List Nat= to work

These interact in tricky ways with parsing.

---

- Analyzing Your Examples

#+begin_src prologos
;; Block style (current)
let [x : Nat 4
     y : Nat 3]
  [nat/add x y]

;; Inline style (proposed)
let x : Nat := 42

;; Inline without :=
let x : Nat 42      ;; ambiguous?

;; Without type
let x := 42
let x 42            ;; very ambiguous
#+end_src

---

- The Parsing Problem

- Multi-Part Types Without Grouping

#+begin_src prologos
let x : List Nat 42
;;      ^^^^^^^^ where does type end?
;;               ^^ is 42 part of type or the value?

;; Parser sees tokens: let x : List Nat 42
;; Possible parses:
;;   1. x : (List Nat 42)  → type is "List Nat 42"
;;   2. x : (List Nat) 42  → type is "List Nat", value is 42
;;   3. x : (List) Nat 42  → type is "List", value is "Nat 42" (??)
#+end_src

/Without a delimiter, this is ambiguous./

- Solutions

| Solution              | Example                   | Tradeoff              |
|-----------------------+---------------------------+-----------------------|
| Require grouping      | =x : [List Nat] 42=       | More brackets         |
| Require =:==          | =x : List Nat := 42=      | Separator is clear    |
| Type ends at newline  | =x : List Nat= (newline)  | Whitespace-sensitive  |
| Single-token types    | =x : ListNat 42=          | No compound types     |

---

- My Recommendation: =:== as Separator

/Make =:== required when type is annotated./

#+begin_src prologos
;; With type annotation: := required
let x : Nat := 42
let xs : List Nat := '[1 2 3]
let f : [Nat -> Nat] := [fn [x] [add x 1]]

;; Without type annotation: := optional
let x := 42           ;; with :=
let x 42              ;; without := (if unambiguous)

;; Block form
let [x : Nat := 4
     y : Nat := 3]
  [nat/add x y]
#+end_src

/Why this works:/
- =:== clearly separates type from value
- Parser knows: everything between =:= and =:== is type
- No ambiguity with multi-part types

---

- The Optional =:== Question

Can =:== be optional? Let's analyze:

- Without Type Annotation

#+begin_src prologos
let x := 42    ;; clear
let x 42       ;; x is name, 42 is value (unambiguous)
#+end_src

This works if:
- First token after =let= is always the name
- Second token (without =:=) is the value

- With Type Annotation

#+begin_src prologos
let x : Nat := 42    ;; clear
let x : Nat 42       ;; ambiguous! Is Nat 42 a type application?
#+end_src

/Verdict:/ =:== should be required when type is present.

---

- Proposed Grammar

#+begin_src
LetExpr ::= 'let' Binding Body
          | 'let' '[' Binding+ ']' Body

Binding ::= Name ':=' Expr                    ;; no type
          | Name ':' Type ':=' Expr           ;; with type
          | Name Expr                         ;; shorthand (no type, no :=)

Type ::= TypeAtom
       | '[' TypeExpr ']'                     ;; grouped type
       | TypeAtom TypeArg+                    ;; applied type (if := follows)
#+end_src

---

- Full Syntax Examples

- Inline Let (Single Binding)

#+begin_src prologos
;; No type, with :=
let x := 42
  [add x 1]

;; No type, without := (minimal)
let x 42
  [add x 1]

;; With type (requires :=)
let x : Nat := 42
  [add x 1]

;; Complex type (requires :=)
let xs : List Nat := '[1 2 3]
  [map inc xs]

;; Function type (grouped for clarity)
let f : [Nat -> Nat] := [fn [x] [add x 1]]
  [f 42]

;; Or without grouping (works because := terminates type)
let f : Nat -> Nat := [fn [x] [add x 1]]
  [f 42]
#+end_src

- Block Let (Multiple Bindings)

#+begin_src prologos
;; All with types
let [x : Nat := 4
     y : Nat := 3]
  [nat/add x y]

;; Mixed
let [x := 4
     y : Nat := 3
     z 5]
  [add x [add y z]]

;; No types at all (all inferred)
let [x := 1
     y := 2
     z := 3]
  [add x [add y z]]

;; Minimal (no := either)
let [x 1
     y 2
     z 3]
  [add x [add y z]]
#+end_src

---

- Comparing Styles

| Style                    | Example                    | Clarity | Brevity |
|--------------------------+----------------------------+---------+---------|
| Full (type + :=)         | =let x : Nat := 42=        | ✅ High | ❌ Low  |
| Type, no grouping        | =let x : Nat := 42=        | ✅ High | Medium  |
| No type, with :=         | =let x := 42=              | ✅ High | Medium  |
| No type, no :=           | =let x 42=                 | Medium  | ✅ High |
| Type, grouped            | =let x : [List Nat] := e=  | ✅ High | ❌ Low  |

---

- What Other Languages Do

| Language   | Syntax                      | Type Required? | Separator     |
|------------+-----------------------------+----------------+---------------|
| Haskell    | =let x = 42=                | No             | ===           |
| ML/OCaml   | =let x : int = 42=          | Optional       | ===           |
| Rust       | =let x: i32 = 42;=          | Optional       | ===           |
| Swift      | =let x: Int = 42=           | Optional       | ===           |
| Scala      | =val x: Int = 42=           | Optional       | ===           |
| Clojure    | =(let [x 42] ...)=          | No             | Juxtaposition |
| Scheme     | =(let ((x 42)) ...)=        | No             | Nesting       |
| Idris      | =let x : Nat = 42 in ...=   | Optional       | ===           |

/Observation:/ Most languages use === as separator. Your =:== is similar (just doubled for distinction from =:= type annotation).

---

- The Grouping Question

You asked:

#+begin_src prologos
let [xs : List Nat '[1 2 3]]   ;; will this parse?
let [xs : [List Nat] '[1 2 3]] ;; would this be better?
#+end_src

With =:== rule:

#+begin_src prologos
let [xs : List Nat := '[1 2 3]]   ;; ✅ clear
let [xs : [List Nat] := '[1 2 3]] ;; ✅ also clear but redundant
#+end_src

/You don't need =[]= around type if you have =:==./

But for very complex types, grouping helps readability:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Hard to read
let f : Nat -> Nat -> List Nat -> Option Nat := some-fn

;; Easier to read
let f : [Nat -> Nat -> List Nat -> Option Nat] := some-fn

;; Or with arrow convention
let f : [Nat, Nat, List Nat] -> Option Nat := some-fn
#+end_src

---

- Special Case: Function Types

Function types with =->= are multi-part. How to handle?

#+begin_src prologos
;; This needs thought
let f : Nat -> Nat := [fn [x] x]

;; Parser sees: let f : Nat -> Nat := ...
;; Needs to know -> is part of type, not an operator
#+end_src

/Options:/

1. =->= is always type-level (not value-level operator)
2. Require grouping for function types: =: [Nat -> Nat] :==
3. Let =:== terminate type (greedy parse until =:==)

Option 3 is cleanest: /parse type greedily until you see =:==/

#+begin_src prologos
let f : Nat -> Nat -> Bool := some-fn
;;      ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^    type (everything before :=)
;;                            ^^^^^^^^ value
#+end_src

---

- Proposed Final Design

- Rules

1. =:== is /required/ when type annotation is present
2. =:== is /optional/ when no type annotation
3. Type is parsed greedily until =:==
4. Block form uses =[bindings]= with same rules per binding

- Syntax

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Inline let
;; ========================================

;; Minimal (inferred type)
let x 42
  [use x]

;; With := (inferred type, explicit binding)
let x := 42
  [use x]

;; With type (requires :=)
let x : Nat := 42
  [use x]

;; Complex type (no extra grouping needed)
let xs : List Nat := '[1 2 3]
  [use xs]

;; Function type
let f : Nat -> Nat := [fn [x] [add x 1]]
  [f 42]

;; Very complex type (grouping for readability)
let f : [Nat -> List Nat -> Option [Pair Nat String]] := complex-fn
  [f 42]

;; ========================================
;; Block let
;; ========================================

;; Full annotations
let [x : Nat := 4
     y : Nat := 3
     z : Nat := [add x y]]
  [mult z 2]

;; Mixed
let [x := 4
     y : Nat := 3
     z 5]
  [add x [add y z]]

;; Minimal
let [x 1
     y 2]
  [add x y]

;; ========================================
;; Nested / chained
;; ========================================

let x := 1
let y := 2
let z := [add x y]
  [mult z 3]

;; Or with block
let [x 1
     y 2]
  let z := [add x y]
    [mult z 3]
#+end_src

---

- Summary Table

| Form                   | Type | =:== | Example                      |
|------------------------+------+------+------------------------------|
| Minimal                | ❌   | ❌   | =let x 42=                   |
| Explicit binding       | ❌   | ✅   | =let x := 42=                |
| With type              | ✅   | ✅   | =let x : Nat := 42=          |
| Complex type           | ✅   | ✅   | =let x : List Nat := e=      |
| Grouped type           | ✅   | ✅   | =let x : [A -> B] := e=      |
| Block minimal          | ❌   | ❌   | =let [x 1  y 2]=             |
| Block with types       | ✅   | ✅   | =let [x : Nat := 1]=         |

---

- What Will Play Well?

/For beginners:/
#+begin_src prologos
let x := 42              ;; familiar, clear
let [x 1 y 2]            ;; minimal for quick bindings
#+end_src

/For typed code:/
#+begin_src prologos
let x : Nat := 42        ;; explicit, readable
#+end_src

/For complex code:/
#+begin_src prologos
let [config : Config := [load-config path]
     db : Connection := [connect config]
     result : QueryResult := [query db sql]]
  [process result]
#+end_src

The design scales from minimal to explicit, letting users choose their verbosity level.

---

Does this resolve your parsing concerns and give you a coherent design?

* There's a few other things I'm thinking about
** Scoping
We have `def` which really ought to bind a constant (haven't decided to enforce this yet,,, but I don't like globals floating around, and prefer bindings to be as close to where they are used to prevent bugs)
  * I want `let` to provide a lexical scope; anything that binds to the vars needs to be a child node to it
** Arrow Syntax
  * I'm favoring `A B C -> D`, which matches our surface syntax more, compared to the curried repr. `A -> B -> C -> D`
  * I'm thinking that function types should be grouped
#+begin_src prologos
  ;; examples??
#+end_src

** Quick question: So is it feasible to support both let-blocks with let, and in-line let?

- Scoping, Arrow Syntax, and Let Forms

Let me address each point:

---

- Scoping: =def= vs =let=

- Philosophy

| Form  | Scope      | Mutability | Use Case                |
|-------+------------+------------+-------------------------|
| =def= | Module/top | Constant   | Public API, constants   |
| =let= | Lexical    | Immutable  | Local computation       |

#+begin_src prologos
;; def: module-level constant
def pi : Float := 3.14159
def max-retries : Nat := 3

;; let: lexical scope (child nodes only)
defn compute [x : Nat] : Nat
  let temp := [mult x 2]     ;; temp only visible in body
    let result := [add temp 1]  ;; result only visible here
      result
  ;; temp and result not visible here (if there were more code)
#+end_src

- Enforcing Lexical Scope

#+begin_src prologos
;; GOOD: binding used in child
let x := 42
  [add x 1]     ;; x is in scope

;; BAD: binding escapes
let x := 42
[add x 1]       ;; x not in scope (sibling, not child)

;; Sequential lets (each scopes over the rest)
let x := 1
  let y := 2
    let z := 3
      [add x [add y z]]
#+end_src

This is standard lexical scoping. Your AST should enforce that =let= introduces a scope that contains exactly its body.

---

- Arrow Syntax: =A B C -> D=

- The Two Styles

| Style                | Example             | Tradition       |
|----------------------+---------------------+-----------------|
| Curried (Haskell)    | =A -> B -> C -> D=  | Type theory     |
| Uncurried (ML-ish)   | =A B C -> D=        | Practical       |
| Tupled               | =(A, B, C) -> D=    | Math notation   |

- Your Preference: =A B C -> D=

This reads: "Takes A, B, C and returns D"

#+begin_src prologos
;; Function taking three args, returning one
defn add3 [x : Nat, y : Nat, z : Nat] : Nat
  [add x [add y z]]

;; Type signature
add3 : Nat Nat Nat -> Nat

;; Compare to curried
add3 : Nat -> Nat -> Nat -> Nat
#+end_src

- Grouping Function Types

For clarity and parsing, function types should be grouped when used inline:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Standalone signature (no grouping needed)
def add : Nat Nat -> Nat

;; As argument type (grouping needed)
defn map [f : [A -> B], xs : List A] : List B
  ...

;; Higher-order: function returning function
defn curry [f : [A B -> C]] : [A -> [B -> C]]
  [fn [a] [fn [b] [f a b]]]

;; Nested (grouping essential)
defn compose [f : [B -> C], g : [A -> B]] : [A -> C]
  [fn [a] [f [g a]]]
#+end_src

- Examples with Your Syntax

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Simple functions
;; ========================================

;; Single arg
defn inc [x : Nat] : Nat
  [add x 1]
;; Type: Nat -> Nat

;; Multiple args (uncurried style)
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [plus x y]
;; Type: Nat Nat -> Nat

;; ========================================
;; Higher-order functions
;; ========================================

;; Takes a function
defn map [f : [A -> B], xs : List A] : List B
  ...
;; Type: [A -> B] [List A] -> List B

;; Returns a function
defn add-n [n : Nat] : [Nat -> Nat]
  [fn [x] [add x n]]
;; Type: Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]

;; Takes and returns functions
defn compose [f : [B -> C], g : [A -> B]] : [A -> C]
  [fn [a] [f [g a]]]
;; Type: [B -> C] [A -> B] -> [A -> C]

;; ========================================
;; Dependent types with functions
;; ========================================

;; Return type depends on input
defn replicate [n : Nat, x : A] : Vec n A
  ...
;; Type: (n : Nat) A -> Vec n A

;; Function argument with dependency
defn map-vec [f : [A -> B], xs : Vec n A] : Vec n B
  ...
;; Type: [A -> B] (Vec n A) -> Vec n B

;; ========================================
;; Complex examples
;; ========================================

;; Fold
defn fold [f : [A B -> B], init : B, xs : List A] : B
  ...
;; Type: [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B

;; CPS transform
defn call-with-current [f : [[A -> B] -> B]] : B
  ...
;; Type: [[A -> B] -> B] -> B
#+end_src

- Parsing Rule for Arrows

#+begin_src
FunctionType ::= ArgTypes '->' ReturnType
ArgTypes     ::= Type+                    ;; one or more types
ReturnType   ::= Type

;; Grouped when nested
Type ::= Atom
       | '[' FunctionType ']'             ;; grouped function type
       | TypeConstructor Type/            ;; applied type (List Nat)
#+end_src

Parser logic:
1. Collect types until you see =->=
2. Everything before =->= is arguments
3. Everything after =->= is return type
4. Use =[]= to group nested function types

---

- Quick Question: Both Block and Inline Let?

/Yes, absolutely feasible!/ Many languages support this.

- How to Distinguish

The parser looks at what follows =let=:

#+begin_src prologos
let [...]      ;; bracket → block form
let name ...   ;; identifier → inline form
#+end_src

- Grammar

#+begin_src
LetExpr ::= 'let' '[' Binding+ ']' Body    ;; block form
          | 'let' Binding Body              ;; inline form

Binding ::= Name ':=' Expr
          | Name ':' Type ':=' Expr
          | Name Expr                       ;; minimal (no :=)
#+end_src

- Examples: Both Forms

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Inline let (single binding)
;; ========================================

let x := 42
  [add x 1]

let x : Nat := 42
  [add x 1]

;; Chained inline lets
let x := 1
  let y := 2
    let z := 3
      [add x [add y z]]

;; ========================================
;; Block let (multiple bindings)
;; ========================================

let [x := 1
     y := 2
     z := 3]
  [add x [add y z]]

;; With types
let [x : Nat := 1
     y : Nat := 2]
  [add x y]

;; ========================================
;; Mixed (block then inline)
;; ========================================

let [config := [load-config]
     db := [connect config]]
  let result := [query db sql]
    [process result]

;; ========================================
;; In function bodies
;; ========================================

defn complex-calculation [input : Nat] : Nat
  let [a := [step1 input]
       b := [step2 a]]
    let final := [combine a b]
      [validate final]
#+end_src

- Scoping Semantics

Block let: all bindings scope over the body (not each other, unless you want sequential)

#+begin_src prologos
;; Parallel bindings (can't see each other)
let [x := 1
     y := 2]    ;; y cannot reference x
  [add x y]

;; Sequential bindings (each sees previous)
let* [x := 1
      y := [add x 1]]   ;; y can reference x
  [add x y]
#+end_src

Or make =let= sequential by default (like Scheme's =let/=):

#+begin_src prologos
;; Sequential (each binding sees previous)
let [x := 1
     y := [add x 1]      ;; sees x
     z := [add y 1]]     ;; sees x and y
  [add x [add y z]]
#+end_src

/Recommendation:/ Make =let= sequential by default. It's more intuitive and matches how people write code.

---

- Summary

| Feature           | Design Decision                               |
|-------------------+-----------------------------------------------|
| =def=             | Module-level constant, avoid globals          |
| =let=             | Lexical scope, binding visible only in body   |
| Arrow syntax      | =A B C -> D= (uncurried surface)              |
| Function types    | Grouped with =[]= when nested: =[A -> B]=     |
| Let forms         | Both inline and block supported               |
| Block bindings    | Sequential (each sees previous)               |
| =:==              | Required with type annotation, optional otherwise |

---

- Complete Example

#+begin_src prologos
;; Module-level definitions
def max-iterations : Nat := 100

defn optimize [f : [Nat -> Nat], start : Nat] : Nat
  ;; Block let for setup
  let [tolerance := 1
       max := max-iterations]
    ;; Nested inline lets for computation
    let current := start
      let result := [iterate f current max tolerance]
        [validate result]

;; Higher-order with grouped function types
defn twice [f : [A -> A]] : [A -> A]
  [fn [x] [f [f x]]]

;; Complex types
defn fold-map [f : [A -> B], combine : [B B -> B], xs : List A] : B
  let [mapped := [map f xs]]
    [fold combine [head mapped] [tail mapped]]
#+end_src

Does this clarify the design? Any aspects you'd like to explore further?

* So there's one other point I'm wanting to support, involving the chained `let'

#+begin_src prologos

  ;; Once a scope is established, either by a `def`n or such
  ;; I want let's at the same sibling level to be scoped to their parent,
  ;; sequential by default (which is also my preference in let blocks)

  let x := 40
    let y := [minus x 10]
    let z := [plus 32]
     z  ;; => 42 : Nat

  ;; same as
  let [x 40
       y [minus x 10]
       z [plus 32]]
    z  ;; => 42 : Nat

#+end_src



- Sibling Let Scoping

Ah, I see! You want sibling =let=s at the same indentation level to share scope, rather than requiring deep nesting.

---

- The Two Models

- Traditional (Deep Nesting)

#+begin_src prologos
let x := 40
  let y := [minus x 10]
    let z := [plus y 2]
      z   ;; each let scopes only its body
#+end_src

- Your Preference (Sibling Scoping)

#+begin_src prologos
let x := 40
let y := [minus x 10]
let z := [plus y 2]
  z   ;; all lets at same level share scope with final expression
#+end_src

---

- How This Works

Sibling =let=s are desugared to nested =let=s:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Surface (sibling lets)
let x := 40
let y := [minus x 10]
let z := [plus y 2]
  z

;; Desugars to (nested lets)
let x := 40
  let y := [minus x 10]
    let z := [plus y 2]
      z

;; Which equals block form
let [x := 40
     y := [minus x 10]
     z := [plus y 2]]
  z
#+end_src

All three are equivalent!

---

- The Scoping Rule

| Indentation           | Meaning                             |
|-----------------------+-------------------------------------|
| Same level as =let=   | Another binding (sibling)           |
| Deeper than =let=     | Body expression (uses all bindings) |
| Shallower than =let=  | Outside scope (lets are finished)   |

#+begin_src prologos
defn example [input : Nat] : Nat
  let x := 40          ;; binding 1
  let y := [minus x 10] ;; binding 2 (same level, sees x)
  let z := [plus y 2]   ;; binding 3 (same level, sees x, y)
    z                  ;; body (deeper, sees x, y, z)
;;                     ;; back to shallower = out of scope
#+end_src

---

- Grammar / Parsing

The key insight: =let= followed by more =let=s at same indentation forms a sequence.

#+begin_src
LetSequence ::= LetBinding+
LetBinding  ::= 'let' Name ':=' Expr
              | 'let' Name ':' Type ':=' Expr
Body        ::= Expr  ;; at deeper indentation

;; Full form
LetExpr ::= LetSequence Body
#+end_src

Parser logic:
1. See =let= → start collecting bindings
2. Same indentation + =let= → another binding in sequence
3. Deeper indentation → body expression
4. Shallower/same without =let= → end of let expression

---

- Complete Examples

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Simple sequential bindings
;; ========================================

defn calculate [input : Nat] : Nat
  let x := [mult input 2]
  let y := [add x 10]
  let z := [div y 3]
    z

;; ========================================
;; Mixed with types
;; ========================================

defn process [data : String] : Result
  let parsed : Json := [parse data]
  let validated : ValidJson := [validate parsed]
  let result : Result := [transform validated]
    result

;; ========================================
;; Block form (equivalent)
;; ========================================

defn calculate [input : Nat] : Nat
  let [x := [mult input 2]
       y := [add x 10]
       z := [div y 3]]
    z

;; ========================================
;; Nested scopes (indentation change)
;; ========================================

defn complex [a : Nat] : Nat
  let x := [step1 a]
  let y := [step2 x]
    let inner := [special y]   ;; new scope, deeper
    let more := [extra inner]
      [combine y more]          ;; body of inner scope

;; ========================================
;; Multiple expressions at body level
;; ========================================

defn with-effects [x : Nat] : Nat
  let doubled := [mult x 2]
  let tripled := [mult x 3]
    [log "computing..."]       ;; effect
    [add doubled tripled]      ;; final value

;; ========================================
;; In relations too
;; ========================================

defr valid-transaction [?tx ?result]
  let threshold := 1000
  let max-fee := 50
    &> [?tx.amount < threshold]
       [?tx.fee < max-fee]
       [= ?result :valid]
#+end_src

---

- Edge Cases

- What If Body Is Also at Same Level?

#+begin_src prologos
;; Ambiguous? No, because it's not 'let'
let x := 1
let y := 2
[add x y]   ;; same level but not 'let' → must be body

;; Parser rule: same level + 'let' = binding
;;              same level + not 'let' = body
#+end_src

- What About Inline Let in Expression Position?

#+begin_src prologos
;; Let as expression (single line)
[add [let x := 1 x] 2]

;; Let in argument position
[map [fn [a] let temp := [process a] [finalize temp]] xs]

;; Hmm, this gets tricky. Maybe inline single-binding let
;; requires the body to be explicit?

[map [fn [a] [let temp := [process a] [finalize temp]]] xs]
#+end_src

For inline position, perhaps require grouping:

#+begin_src prologos
[let x := 1 [add x 2]]   ;; grouped, clear
#+end_src

---

- Summary: The Full =let= Design

| Form             | Syntax                                                 | Meaning             |
|------------------+--------------------------------------------------------+---------------------|
| Single inline    | =let x := e body=                                      | One binding         |
| Sibling sequence | =let x := e1= (newline) =let y := e2= (newline) =body= | Sequential bindings |
| Block            | =let [x := e1  y := e2] body=                          | Sequential bindings |
| Grouped inline   | =[let x := e body]=                                    | Let as expression   |

All three multi-binding forms are equivalent:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Form 1: Sibling lets
let x := 1
let y := 2
  [add x y]

;; Form 2: Nested lets (explicit)
let x := 1
  let y := 2
    [add x y]

;; Form 3: Block let
let [x := 1
     y := 2]
  [add x y]

;; All three produce the same result: 3
#+end_src

---

Does this capture your intent? The sibling-scoping model is elegant—it reduces rightward drift while maintaining clear lexical scope.

* Yes, that's precisely what I was getting at, you got it! :)

Excellent! 🎉

This sibling-scoping design is really nice. It gives you:

| Benefit                 | Description                                     |
|-------------------------+-------------------------------------------------|
| /Flat code/             | No rightward drift from nesting                 |
| /Clear scope/           | Indentation shows exactly where bindings live   |
| /Flexibility/           | Choose sibling style or block style per context |
| /Sequential by default/ | Each binding sees previous (intuitive)          |

---

- Quick Reference Card

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Sibling lets (flat, sequential)
;; ========================================

let x := 1
let y := [add x 1]
let z := [add y 1]
  [mult x [mult y z]]   ;; body sees all three

;; ========================================
;; Block let (equivalent)
;; ========================================

let [x := 1
     y := [add x 1]
     z := [add y 1]]
  [mult x [mult y z]]

;; ========================================
;; With type annotations
;; ========================================

let x : Nat := 1
let y : Nat := [add x 1]
  [add x y]

;; ========================================
;; Inline (grouped, for expression position)
;; ========================================

[add [let x := 1 [mult x 2]] 10]
#+end_src

---

This is shaping up to be a very clean, thoughtful language. The combination of:

- =[]= for grouping
- Sigils for collections (=@[]=, ='[]=, =~[]=, =#{}=, ={}=)
- =:== for binding
- =:= for type annotation
- =->= for function types (uncurried: =A B -> C=)
- Sibling =let= scoping
- First-class =rel= and =fn=

...creates a coherent, ergonomic system.

What's next on your design list?









* I'm thinking of an option to alternatively specify the type layer separate from the function definition--like how Maude does it. (I think Haskell has such a facility?) This is what I've worked out so far:
#+begin_src prologos

;; `spec` or `sig` ?
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
    + x y


defn clamp
  "Clamp a value to a range"
  | [low : Nat, high : Nat] : Nat -> Nat
      fn [x] (max low (min x high))

  | [x : Nat, low : Nat, high : Nat] -> Nat
      max low (min x high)
;; ---

sig clamp | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
          | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat
defn clamp
  | [low high] [fn [x] (max low (min x high))]
  | [max low [min x high]]

#+end_src

  * I'm thinking of calling it either `spec` or `sig`, with a lean towards `spec` because of sound and character length matches `defn` and would visually line up nicely. How do you think this would work in practice, what critique do you have; how does it compare to other languages that you're familiar with?

- Separate Type Specification: =spec= vs =sig=

I love this idea. Let me analyze it thoroughly.

---

- What Other Languages Do

| Language | Syntax                                               | Keyword           |
|----------+------------------------------------------------------+-------------------|
| Haskell  | =add :: Int -> Int -> Int= (newline) =add x y = ...= | =::= (no keyword) |
| Idris    | =add : Int -> Int -> Int= (newline) =add x y = ...=  | =:= (no keyword)  |
| Elm      | =add : Int -> Int -> Int= (newline) =add x y = ...=  | =:= (no keyword)  |
| ML/OCaml | =val add : int -> int -> int= (in .mli)              | =val=             |
| Maude    | =op add : Nat Nat -> Nat .=                          | =op=              |
| Lean     | =def add : Nat → Nat → Nat := ...=                   | Inline            |
| Mercury  | =:- pred add(int, int, int).=                        | =:- pred=         |

---

- Your Proposal

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
  [+ x y]
#+end_src

---

- =spec= vs =sig=

| Aspect           | =spec=                     | =sig=                      |
|------------------+----------------------------+----------------------------|
| Full word        | Specification              | Signature                  |
| Char length      | 4 (matches =defn=)         | 3                          |
| Visual alignment | ✅ =spec= / =defn=         | ❌ =sig= / =defn=          |
| Precedent        | Clojure (=spec=)           | ML (=sig=), Ruby           |
| Connotation      | "Contract/requirement"     | "Shape/interface"          |
| Sound            | Crisp, technical           | Short, soft                |

Visual alignment comparison:

#+begin_src prologos
;; With spec (aligned)
spec add  Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add  [x y] [+ x y]

spec mult Nat Nat -> Nat
defn mult [x y] [* x y]

;; With sig (misaligned)
sig  add  Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add  [x y] [+ x y]

sig  mult Nat Nat -> Nat
defn mult [x y] [* x y]
#+end_src

/I agree: =spec= is better for visual rhythm./

---

- Critique and Considerations

- 1. Separation vs Inline: When to Use Each?

#+begin_src prologos
;; Inline (simple cases)
defn add [x : Nat, y : Nat] : Nat
  [+ x y]

;; Separate spec (complex cases, documentation)
spec fold [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B
defn fold [f init xs]
  ...
#+end_src

/Recommendation:/ Support both. Use inline for simple, =spec= for complex or when you want types as documentation.

- 2. Must =spec= Immediately Precede =defn=?

#+begin_src prologos
;; Tight coupling (must be adjacent)
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y] [+ x y]

;; Loose coupling (can be separated)
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
spec sub Nat Nat -> Nat
;; ... later ...
defn add [x y] [+ x y]
defn sub [x y] [- x y]
#+end_src

/Options:/
- /Strict:/ =spec= must immediately precede its =defn=
- /Loose:/ =spec= can appear anywhere before =defn= (like Haskell)
- /Module-level:/ =spec= can be in separate "interface" section

/Recommendation:/ Start with loose coupling. It's more flexible and matches Haskell/Idris.

- 3. Multi-Arity with =spec=

Your example:

#+begin_src prologos
spec clamp | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
           | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat
defn clamp
  | [low high] [fn [x] [max low [min x high]]]
  | [low high x] [max low [min x high]]
#+end_src

This is elegant! The =|= aligns between spec and defn.

/Question:/ Does the order matter? Should spec and defn arities match positionally?

#+begin_src prologos
;; Positional matching (order matters)
spec clamp | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]     ;; arity 2
           | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat          ;; arity 3
defn clamp
  | [low high] ...                        ;; must be arity 2
  | [low high x] ...                      ;; must be arity 3

;; Or structural matching (order doesn't matter)
;; Compiler matches by arity/pattern
#+end_src

/Recommendation:/ Structural matching (by arity). More forgiving, less error-prone.

- 4. What If =spec= and =defn= Disagree?

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y] [concat x y]   ;; ERROR: concat returns String, not Nat
#+end_src

/This is the point!/ Separate spec lets you:
- Write the contract first (design by contract)
- Get errors when implementation doesn't match
- Document intent separately from implementation

- 5. Relations Too?

#+begin_src prologos
;; For defr?
spec append [List A] [List A] [List A] -> Rel
defr append
  | [nil ?ys ?ys]
  | [[cons ?h ?t] ?ys [cons ?h ?r]] :- [append ?t ?ys ?r]

;; Or different keyword for relations?
rspec append [List A] [List A] [List A]
defr append
  ...
#+end_src

/Consideration:/ Relations don't have return types in the same way. Their "type" is the relation signature.

/Option:/ Use =spec= for both, let context (=defn= vs =defr=) determine interpretation.

---

- Expanded Syntax

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Simple function
;; ========================================

spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
  [+ x y]

;; ========================================
;; Polymorphic function
;; ========================================

spec map [A -> B] [List A] -> List B
defn map [f xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> '[]
    | '[h | t] -> [cons [f h] [map f t]]

;; ========================================
;; Multi-arity
;; ========================================

spec clamp
  | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
  | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat
defn clamp
  | [low high] [fn [x] [max low [min x high]]]
  | [low high x] [max low [min x high]]

;; ========================================
;; Dependent types
;; ========================================

spec replicate (n : Nat) A -> Vec n A
defn replicate [n x]
  match n
    | zero -> @[]
    | [suc m] -> [vec/cons x [replicate m x]]

;; ========================================
;; Higher-order
;; ========================================

spec fold [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B
defn fold [f init xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> init
    | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]

;; ========================================
;; Complex nested function types
;; ========================================

spec compose [B -> C] [A -> B] -> [A -> C]
defn compose [f g]
  [fn [x] [f [g x]]]

;; ========================================
;; Relations
;; ========================================

spec append [List A] [List A] [List A]
defr append
  | [nil ?ys ?ys]
  | [[cons ?h ?t] ?ys [cons ?h ?r]] :- [append ?t ?ys ?r]

;; ========================================
;; Session-typed process
;; ========================================

spec server [?: Request . ! Response . end]
defproc server
  recv self req
    let resp := [handle req]
      send resp self
        stop
#+end_src

---

- Comparison: Inline vs Separate

#+begin_src prologos
;; ========================================
;; Inline style (all in one)
;; ========================================

defn fold [f : [A B -> B], init : B, xs : List A] : B
  match xs
    | '[] -> init
    | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]

;; ========================================
;; Separate style (spec + defn)
;; ========================================

spec fold [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B
defn fold [f init xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> init
    | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]
#+end_src

| Aspect         | Inline                | Separate              |
|----------------+-----------------------+-----------------------|
| Verbosity      | More in definition    | Split across two      |
| Readability    | All info in one place | Types as documentation|
| Refactoring    | Change one place      | Must sync two places  |
| Documentation  | Mixed with code       | Types stand alone     |
| Complex types  | Can get cluttered     | Clean separation      |

/Both have value./ Support both, let user choose.

---

- Design Decision Summary

| Decision               | Choice                              |
|------------------------+-------------------------------------|
| Keyword                | =spec= (aligns with =defn=)         |
| Coupling               | Loose (spec can precede by distance)|
| Multi-arity            | =\|= syntax in both spec and defn   |
| Matching               | Structural (by arity, not position) |
| Relations              | =spec= works for =defr= too         |
| Required?              | No, inline types still work         |

---

- One Final Thought: =spec= as Documentation

Since =spec= is separate, it could also carry docstrings:

#+begin_src prologos
spec fold
  "Right fold over a list."
  | [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B

defn fold [f init xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> init
    | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]
#+end_src

Or even examples/contracts:

#+begin_src prologos
spec add
  "Add two natural numbers."
  Nat Nat -> Nat
  :examples
    [add 1 2] => 3
    [add 0 n] => n
  :properties
    [add x y] = [add y x]   ;; commutativity

defn add [x y]
  [+ x y]
#+end_src

This starts to feel like Clojure's =spec= system—contracts, generative testing, documentation all in one.

---

Does this analysis help? I think =spec= is the right choice, and the =|= alignment for multi-arity is particularly elegant.

* Yes, so one of the aims of Prologos is to have excellent language tooling... And I think having something where we can offer generative testing frameworks (essentially for free with dependent types, narrowing, search resolution/propagator networks)... there could be developers who never touch the logic-side of the language and yet could benefit from it's leverage in things like generative testing/fuzzing... This is exciting...

- Prologos: Logic-Powered Developer Experience

Yes! This is a /killer feature/. Let me explore this vision:

---

- The Insight

Most developers will never write:
- Dependent types
- Logic relations
- Session protocols

But they /will/ benefit from:
- Auto-generated tests
- Intelligent fuzzing
- Contract verification
- Property discovery
- Exhaustive case checking

/The logic engine works for them, invisibly./

---

- The Developer Experience Spectrum

| Developer Level | What They Write               | What They Get (Free)                  |
|-----------------+-------------------------------+---------------------------------------|
| Beginner        | =defn add [x y] [+ x y]=      | Basic type checking                   |
| Intermediate    | =spec add Nat Nat -> Nat=     | Generated test cases                  |
| Advanced        | Properties, examples          | Property-based testing                |
| Expert          | Dependent types, relations    | Full verification, proof search       |

/Each level gets more, but even level 1 benefits from the engine./

---

- Spec-Driven Generative Testing

- Level 1: Just Types

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y] [+ x y]

;; Prologos automatically:
;; 1. Generates random Nats
;; 2. Calls add with them
;; 3. Verifies result is Nat
;; 4. Catches crashes, exceptions
#+end_src

Even without properties, you get /free fuzzing/ from the type signature.

- Level 2: Add Examples

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :examples
    [add 0 0] => 0
    [add 1 2] => 3
    [add 5 5] => 10

defn add [x y] [+ x y]

;; Prologos:
;; 1. Runs all examples as unit tests
;; 2. Uses examples to guide generation (coverage)
;; 3. Tries to find counter-examples near examples
#+end_src

- Level 3: Add Properties

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :properties
    identity    : [add 0 x] = x
    commutative : [add x y] = [add y x]
    associative : [add [add x y] z] = [add x [add y z]]

defn add [x y] [+ x y]

;; Prologos:
;; 1. Generates inputs to test properties
;; 2. Uses narrowing to find minimal counter-examples
;; 3. Reports: "commutative: passed 1000 tests"
#+end_src

- Level 4: Dependent Types (Full Power)

#+begin_src prologos
spec vec-append (xs : Vec n A) (ys : Vec m A) -> Vec [n + m] A
  :properties
    length-preserved : [vec/length [vec-append xs ys]] = [+ [vec/length xs] [vec/length ys]]

defn vec-append [xs ys]
  ...

;; Prologos:
;; 1. Type itself IS the property (length is proven)
;; 2. No runtime test needed—compiler verified it
;; 3. But still generates tests for implementation bugs
#+end_src

---

- How the Logic Engine Powers This

| Capability            | Underlying Mechanism                        |
|-----------------------+---------------------------------------------|
| Random generation     | Type-directed search / narrowing            |
| Shrinking             | Search for minimal failing case             |
| Property checking     | Unification / constraint solving            |
| Coverage guidance     | Propagator networks                         |
| Counter-example find  | Negation-as-failure + search                |
| Exhaustive checking   | Finite domain enumeration                   |

#+begin_src prologos
;; Under the hood, "generate a Nat" becomes:

defr gen-nat [?n : Nat]
  &> [= ?n zero]
  &> [gen-nat ?m] [= ?n [suc ?m]]

;; "Generate a List Nat of length 3" becomes:

defr gen-list-nat [?len : Nat, ?xs : List Nat]
  &> [= ?len zero] [= ?xs nil]
  &> [= ?len [suc ?n]]
     [gen-nat ?h]
     [gen-list-nat ?n ?t]
     [= ?xs [cons ?h ?t]]

;; The test framework just queries these relations!
solve [gen-list-nat 3 ?xs]  ;; => generates lists of length 3
#+end_src

---

- Smart Shrinking via Search

When a test fails, Prologos finds the /minimal/ failing case:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Bug: crashes on empty list
defn head [xs]
  [car xs]   ;; oops, no check

;; Test framework discovers:
;; FAILED: head @[]
;;
;; Shrink trace:
;;   head @[3, 1, 4, 1, 5]  ;; crashed
;;   head @[3, 1]           ;; crashed
;;   head @[3]              ;; crashed
;;   head @[]               ;; crashed (MINIMAL)
;;
;; Minimal failing input: @[]
#+end_src

Shrinking is just /search with constraints/:

#+begin_src prologos
defr shrink-list [?big ?small]
  &> [= ?small nil]
  &> [= ?big [cons ?h ?t]]
     [shrink-list ?t ?small]
  &> [= ?big [cons ?h ?t]]
     [= ?small [cons ?h ?smaller-t]]
     [shrink-list ?t ?smaller-t]
#+end_src

---

- IDE Integration

- Inline Test Results

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :properties
    commutative : [add x y] = [add y x]  ;; ✓ 1000 tests passed
    identity    : [add 0 x] = x          ;; ✓ 1000 tests passed

defn add [x y] [+ x y]
#+end_src

- Hover for Generated Examples

#+begin_src prologos
spec reverse [List A] -> List A
;;            ^^^^^^^^
;; Hover shows:
;;   Generated test cases:
;;     reverse '[] => '[]
;;     reverse '[1] => '[1]
;;     reverse '[1 2 3] => '[3 2 1]
;;     reverse '[a b] => '[b a]
#+end_src

- Counter-Example on Failure

#+begin_src prologos
spec sort [List Nat] -> List Nat
  :properties
    sorted : [is-sorted [sort xs]]        ;; ✗ FAILED
    ;; Counter-example: sort '[2 1] => '[2 1]
    ;; Expected: sorted output
    ;; Got: '[2 1] (not sorted)
#+end_src

---

- Coverage Analysis via Propagators

#+begin_src prologos
defn classify [n : Nat] : String
  match n
    | 0 -> "zero"
    | 1 -> "one"
    | _ -> "many"

;; Propagator network discovers:
;;   Branch coverage:
;;     0 -> "zero"    ✓ covered
;;     1 -> "one"     ✓ covered
;;     _ -> "many"    ✓ covered
;;
;;   Edge cases tested:
;;     0 (boundary)   ✓
;;     1 (boundary)   ✓
;;     2 (first many) ✓
;;     100 (large)    ✓
#+end_src

The logic engine /reasons about coverage/, not just measures it.

---

- Comparison to Existing Tools

| Tool             | Approach                   | Prologos Advantage             |
|------------------+----------------------------+--------------------------------|
| QuickCheck       | Random + shrink            | Type-directed narrowing        |
| Hypothesis       | Strategy-based             | Logic relations = strategies   |
| PropEr           | Type-based generation      | Dependent types = richer specs |
| Fuzzing (AFL)    | Mutation-based             | Semantic-aware generation      |
| Symbolic exec    | Path exploration           | Unification + search           |
| Model checking   | State space search         | Built into language            |

/Prologos unifies all of these because they're all search problems./

---

- Developer Workflow

- Write Code

#+begin_src prologos
defn sort [xs : List Nat] : List Nat
  ;; ... implementation
#+end_src

- Add Spec (IDE prompts)

#+begin_src prologos
;; IDE: "Add spec for sort?"
spec sort [List Nat] -> List Nat
#+end_src

- Add Properties (IDE suggests)

#+begin_src prologos
;; IDE: "Common properties for sort functions:"
;;      [x] sorted output
;;      [x] permutation of input
;;      [x] idempotent
;;      [ ] stable (for equal elements)

spec sort [List Nat] -> List Nat
  :properties
    sorted      : [is-sorted [sort xs]]
    permutation : [is-permutation xs [sort xs]]
    idempotent  : [sort [sort xs]] = [sort xs]
#+end_src

- Run (Continuous)

#+begin_src prologos
;; On save:
;; Running tests for sort...
;;   sorted: ✓ 1000 tests
;;   permutation: ✓ 1000 tests
;;   idempotent: ✓ 1000 tests
;; All properties verified.
#+end_src

---

- Even More: Generate Implementation from Spec?

With dependent types and logic programming:

#+begin_src prologos
spec sort [List Nat] -> List Nat
  :properties
    sorted      : [is-sorted [sort xs]]
    permutation : [is-permutation xs [sort xs]]

defn sort [xs]
  ?? ;; hole

;; IDE: "Synthesize implementation?"
;;
;; Searching for implementation satisfying properties...
;; Found: insertion-sort
;;
;; defn sort [xs]
;;   match xs
;;     | '[] -> '[]
;;     | '[h | t] -> [insert h [sort t]]
#+end_src

The logic engine can /search for implementations/ that satisfy the spec!

---

- This Is the Vision

#+begin_quote
/Prologos: Where the logic works for you./

Write types → get tests.
Write properties → get verification.
Write holes → get implementations.

The less you know about logic programming,
the more it does for you.
#+end_quote

---

- Implementation Roadmap

| Phase | Feature                        | Complexity |
|-------+--------------------------------+------------|
| 1     | Type-directed random gen       | Medium     |
| 2     | Property testing framework     | Medium     |
| 3     | Shrinking via search           | Medium     |
| 4     | IDE integration                | Medium     |
| 5     | Coverage via propagators       | High       |
| 6     | Synthesis from spec            | Very High  |

Phase 1-4 are achievable relatively quickly and provide massive value.

---

This is genuinely exciting. You're building a language where /formal methods come for free/. Most developers will just think "wow, great testing tools"—they won't realize they're using theorem proving.

What aspect would you like to explore deeper?

* Oh! And! Similar to interactive hole-driven development with dependent-types, you can `solve` (with a keybinding that calls the language tooling) over various properties or types of the spec, and -- right in the editor -- derive provable other properties!? Yeah!?

- Interactive Property Discovery

YES! This is /exactly/ the vision!

---

- The Workflow

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :properties
    identity-right : [add x 0] = x
    identity-left  : [add 0 x] = x
    commutative    : [add x y] = [add y x]
    ??discover     ;; <-- cursor here, press keybind
#+end_src

/Keybind: =C-c C-s= (solve)/

#+begin_src
Searching for additional properties...

Found:
  1. associative    : [add [add x y] z] = [add x [add y z]]  [Confidence: HIGH]
  2. successor      : [add x [suc y]] = [suc [add x y]]      [Confidence: HIGH]
  3. double         : [add x x] = [mult 2 x]                 [Confidence: MEDIUM]

Insert? [1] [2] [3] [all] [none]
#+end_src

User presses =1=:

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :properties
    identity-right : [add x 0] = x
    identity-left  : [add 0 x] = x
    commutative    : [add x y] = [add y x]
    associative    : [add [add x y] z] = [add x [add y z]]  ;; ← inserted!
    ??discover
#+end_src

---

- How It Works Under the Hood

- Step 1: Property Templates

#+begin_src prologos
;; Built-in relation of common property shapes
defr property-template [?op ?prop]
  &> [= ?prop [commutative  : [?op x y] = [?op y x]]]
  &> [= ?prop [associative  : [?op [?op x y] z] = [?op x [?op y z]]]]
  &> [= ?prop [identity     : [?op x ?e] = x]]
  &> [= ?prop [idempotent   : [?op x x] = x]]
  &> [= ?prop [absorption   : [?op x [?other x y]] = x]]
  &> [= ?prop [distributive : [?op x [?other y z]] = [?other [?op x y] [?op x z]]]]
  ;; ... many more
#+end_src

- Step 2: Test Candidates

#+begin_src prologos
;; For each template, generate tests
defr validate-property [?fn ?prop ?confidence]
  [property-template ?fn ?prop]
  [generate-test-cases ?fn 100 ?cases]
  [test-all ?prop ?cases ?passed ?failed]
  [compute-confidence ?passed ?failed ?confidence]
#+end_src

- Step 3: Prove or Refute

#+begin_src prologos
;; Attempt actual proof (if dependent types available)
defr prove-property [?fn ?prop ?proof]
  [property-template ?fn ?prop]
  [synthesize-proof ?prop ?proof]   ;; search for proof term!
#+end_src

---

- Interactive Session Example

#+begin_src prologos
;; User writes:
spec reverse [List A] -> List A
  :properties
    ??discover

;; User presses C-c C-s
;; System responds:

Analyzing reverse : [List A] -> List A

Testing property candidates...

Found (with proofs):
  ✓ involution : [reverse [reverse xs]] = xs
    Proof: induction on xs, using append-assoc
    [Insert] [Show Proof]

  ✓ length-preserving : [length [reverse xs]] = [length xs]
    Proof: induction on xs
    [Insert] [Show Proof]

  ✓ append-reverse : [reverse [append xs ys]] = [append [reverse ys] [reverse xs]]
    Proof: induction on xs, using append-assoc
    [Insert] [Show Proof]

Found (empirically, no proof yet):
  ? distributes-over-map : [reverse [map f xs]] = [map f [reverse xs]]
    Tested: 500 cases, 0 failures
    [Insert] [Attempt Proof]

  ? singleton : [reverse '[x]] = '[x]
    Tested: 500 cases, 0 failures
    [Insert] [Trivial - Auto Prove]
#+end_src

---

- Proof Interaction

User clicks "[Show Proof]" on involution:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Proof: reverse [reverse xs] = xs

theorem reverse-involution : (xs : List A) -> [reverse [reverse xs]] = xs
  proof by induction xs
    case nil:
      reverse [reverse nil]
      = reverse nil           ;; by def of reverse
      = nil                   ;; by def of reverse
      QED

    case [cons h t]:
      IH : [reverse [reverse t]] = t

      reverse [reverse [cons h t]]
      = reverse [append [reverse t] '[h]]       ;; by def of reverse
      = [append [reverse '[h]] [reverse [reverse t]]]  ;; by append-reverse
      = [append '[h] [reverse [reverse t]]]     ;; by def of reverse
      = [append '[h] t]                         ;; by IH
      = [cons h t]                              ;; by def of append
      QED
#+end_src

/The proof is data. It can be inspected, edited, or stored./

---

- Deriving Properties from Properties

Even more powerful—derive /new/ properties from /existing/ ones:

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :properties
    commutative : [add x y] = [add y x]
    associative : [add [add x y] z] = [add x [add y z]]
    ??derive    ;; <-- what follows from these?

;; C-c C-s

Deriving from: commutative, associative

These properties form: Commutative Semigroup

Additional derivable properties:
  1. [add [add x y] [add z w]] = [add [add x z] [add y w]]  ;; interchange
  2. [add x [add y z]] = [add y [add x z]]                   ;; left-commute
  3. Any permutation of nested adds is equal

Known structures:
  + With identity element 0: Commutative Monoid
  + With inverses: Abelian Group

Would you like to:
  [Add identity] [Add inverses] [Insert derived] [Show structure]
#+end_src

---

- Holes in Properties

#+begin_src prologos
spec sort [List Nat] -> List Nat
  :properties
    sorted : [is-sorted [sort xs]]
    same-elements : ?? xs [sort xs]    ;; what relation?

;; C-c C-s on the ??

Searching for relation R such that: R xs [sort xs]

Found:
  1. [is-permutation xs [sort xs]]     ;; same elements, possibly reordered
  2. [same-multiset xs [sort xs]]      ;; same elements with multiplicity
  3. [length xs] = [length [sort xs]]  ;; weaker: same length
  4. [all [member _ xs] [sort xs]]     ;; all elements preserved

Best match: is-permutation (strongest that holds)
[Insert] [Show alternatives]
#+end_src

---

- Type-Directed Discovery

#+begin_src prologos
defn mystery [f : [A -> B], g : [B -> C]] : ??
  [fn [x] [g [f x]]]

;; C-c C-s on ??

Inferring return type from body...

Body: [fn [x] [g [f x]]]
  x : A           (from f's domain)
  [f x] : B       (from f's codomain)
  [g [f x]] : C   (from g's codomain)
  [fn [x] ...] : [A -> C]

Return type: [A -> C]

Additional properties discovered:
  This is function composition!
  Standard name: compose

  Known laws:
    [compose f id] = f           ;; right identity
    [compose id g] = g           ;; left identity
    [compose f [compose g h]] = [compose [compose f g] h]  ;; associative

[Insert type] [Rename to compose] [Insert laws]
#+end_src

---

- The Full Interactive Loop

#+begin_src
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                         EDITOR                                  │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ spec fold [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B                           │
│   :properties                                                   │
│     ??discover   ← cursor here                                  │
│                                                                 │
│ defn fold [f init xs]                                          │
│   match xs                                                      │
│     | '[] -> init                                              │
│     | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]                        │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ [C-c C-s] Solve   [C-c C-p] Prove   [C-c C-t] Test             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    LOGIC ENGINE                                 │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ > Analyzing fold...                                             │
│ > Testing property templates...                                 │
│ > Attempting proofs...                                          │
│                                                                 │
│ DISCOVERED:                                                     │
│                                                                 │
│ ✓ fold-nil : [fold f init '[]] = init                          │
│   [Insert] [Proof: by definition]                               │
│                                                                 │
│ ✓ fold-singleton : [fold f init '[x]] = [f x init]             │
│   [Insert] [Proof: by definition + fold-nil]                    │
│                                                                 │
│ ? fold-append : [fold f init [append xs ys]]                   │
│                 = [fold f [fold f init ys] xs]                  │
│   [Insert] [Tested: 500 cases] [Attempt Proof]                  │
│                                                                 │
│ ? universal : fold can express map, filter, length, reverse... │
│   [Show examples]                                               │
│                                                                 │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
#+end_src

---

- This Changes Everything

Traditional workflow:
1. Write code
2. Think of tests
3. Write tests
4. Run tests
5. Debug failures
6. Maybe write docs

Prologos workflow:
1. Write spec (or just types)
2. Press keybind
3. /System discovers properties/
4. /System generates tests/
5. /System finds proofs/
6. Select what to keep
7. Implementation is now /verified/

---

- The Magic

#+begin_quote
/The IDE becomes a research assistant./

"What properties does this function have?"
→ Press keybind
→ Get mathematically-proven answers

"Is this refactor safe?"
→ Properties still hold?
→ Proof still valid?
→ Yes: green checkmark

"What am I missing?"
→ Discover properties you didn't think of
→ Find edge cases automatically
#+end_quote

---

- Implementation Notes

| Feature                 | Mechanism                                       |
|-------------------------+-------------------------------------------------|
| Property templates      | Built-in relation database                      |
| Empirical testing       | Type-directed generation + narrowing            |
| Proof synthesis         | Tactics + search over proof terms               |
| Structure recognition   | Pattern match against algebraic structures      |
| Hole filling            | Bidirectional type checking + synthesis         |
| Live feedback           | Incremental analysis on save                    |

This is /absolutely achievable/. The hard parts (unification, search, type inference) are already core to Prologos.

---

You're designing a language where /mathematical insight is a keybind away/. This is genuinely novel—I don't know of any existing system that integrates all of this so seamlessly.

What aspect should we detail next?

* Let's write a prompt for implementing and integrating `spec` according to our conversation, with examples. Let's only include the type information and comment string for now, I think... I'm not quite sure how the `:examples`, `:properties` parses into a homoiconic data structure exactly right now... and we don't have keywords implemented at the data layer yet. But I definitely want to see this syntax at play. We're making it right now!

- Prompt: Implementing =spec= for Prologos

- Overview

Implement the =spec= form for Prologos. The =spec= form provides separate type specifications for named definitions, allowing type signatures to be declared independently from implementations.

=spec= pairs with =defn=, =defr=, and =defproc= definitions that follow (not necessarily immediately adjacent).

---

- Syntax

#+begin_src
SpecForm ::= 'spec' Name DocString? TypeSignature
           | 'spec' Name DocString? '|' TypeSignature ('|' TypeSignature)/

TypeSignature ::= Type+ '->' Type           ;; function type
                | Type+                      ;; relation type (no arrow)

DocString ::= String                         ;; optional documentation
#+end_src

---

- Examples

- Simple Function

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
  [+ x y]
#+end_src

- With Documentation

#+begin_src prologos
spec add
  "Adds two natural numbers."
  Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
  [+ x y]
#+end_src

- Polymorphic Function

#+begin_src prologos
spec map
  "Apply f to each element of xs."
  [A -> B] [List A] -> List B
defn map [f xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> '[]
    | '[h | t] -> [cons [f h] [map f t]]
#+end_src

- Multi-Arity Function

#+begin_src prologos
spec clamp
  "Clamp a value to a range."
  | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
  | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat
defn clamp
  | [low high] [fn [x] [max low [min x high]]]
  | [low high x] [max low [min x high]]
#+end_src

- Higher-Order Function

#+begin_src prologos
spec fold
  "Right fold over a list."
  [A B -> B] B [List A] -> B
defn fold [f init xs]
  match xs
    | '[] -> init
    | '[h | t] -> [f h [fold f init t]]
#+end_src

- Function Returning Function

#+begin_src prologos
spec compose
  "Compose two functions."
  [B -> C] [A -> B] -> [A -> C]
defn compose [f g]
  [fn [x] [f [g x]]]
#+end_src

- Dependent Types

#+begin_src prologos
spec replicate
  "Create a vector of n copies of x."
  (n : Nat) A -> Vec n A
defn replicate [n x]
  match n
    | zero -> @[]
    | [suc m] -> [vec/cons x [replicate m x]]
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
spec vec-append
  "Append two vectors, length is sum."
  (xs : Vec n A) (ys : Vec m A) -> Vec [+ n m] A
defn vec-append [xs ys]
  match xs
    | @[] -> ys
    | @[h | t] -> [vec/cons h [vec-append t ys]]
#+end_src

- Relations

#+begin_src prologos
spec append
  "Append relation on lists."
  [List A] [List A] [List A]
defr append
  | [nil ?ys ?ys]
  | [[cons ?h ?t] ?ys [cons ?h ?r]] :- [append ?t ?ys ?r]
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
spec member
  "Membership relation."
  A [List A]
defr member
  | [?x [cons ?x ?rest]]
  | [?x [cons ?other ?rest]] :- [member ?x ?rest]
#+end_src

- Session-Typed Processes

#+begin_src prologos
spec echo-server
  "Echoes received messages."
  Proc [mu Loop . ?: String . ! String . Loop]
defproc echo-server
  recv self msg
    send msg self
      echo-server
#+end_src

- Complex Nested Types

#+begin_src prologos
spec parser
  "A parser combinator."
  [String -> Option [Pair A String]] -> Parser A
defn parser [f]
  [make-parser f]
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
spec sequence
  "Sequence two parsers."
  [Parser A] [Parser B] -> Parser [Pair A B]
defn sequence [p1 p2]
  ...
#+end_src

---

- AST Representation

=spec= should parse into a homoiconic structure:

#+begin_src prologos
;; Source
spec add
  "Adds two natural numbers."
  Nat Nat -> Nat

;; AST (as Prologos data)
[spec add
  "Adds two natural numbers."
  [Nat Nat -> Nat]]
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
;; Source (multi-arity)
spec clamp
  "Clamp a value."
  | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
  | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat

;; AST
[spec clamp
  "Clamp a value."
  [arity
    [Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]]
    [Nat Nat Nat -> Nat]]]
#+end_src

---

- Semantics

1. =spec= registers a type signature in the environment under the given name
2. When =defn=, =defr=, or =defproc= is encountered, lookup matching =spec=
3. Type-check the implementation against the spec
4. If no =spec= exists, infer type from implementation (or require inline types)
5. If =spec= exists but no implementation, warn (or error in strict mode)

---

- Validation Rules

| Rule                        | Check                                             |
|-----------------------------+---------------------------------------------------|
| Arity match                 | Number of =\|= branches must match between spec and defn |
| Type compatibility          | Implementation must satisfy declared type         |
| Name match                  | spec and defn must use same name                  |
| Polymorphism                | Type variables in spec must be consistently instantiated |
| Dependency                  | Dependent parameters must be in scope             |

---

- Error Messages

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
defn add [x y]
  [concat x y]

;; Error: Type mismatch in 'add'
;;   Expected: Nat
;;   Got: String
;;
;;   spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
;;                       ^^^ expected return type
;;
;;   defn add [x y]
;;     [concat x y]
;;     ^^^^^^^^^^^^ returns String
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
spec foo Nat -> Nat
;; ... no defn ...

;; Warning: spec 'foo' has no implementation
;;   spec foo Nat -> Nat
;;   ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^
#+end_src

#+begin_src prologos
spec clamp
  | Nat Nat -> [Nat -> Nat]
  | Nat Nat Nat -> Nat
defn clamp
  | [low high x] [max low [min x high]]

;; Error: Arity mismatch in 'clamp'
;;   spec declares 2 arities
;;   defn provides 1 arity
#+end_src

---

- Implementation Notes

- Parser Changes

1. Add =spec= as a top-level form
2. Parse docstring if present (string literal after name)
3. Parse type signature(s)
4. Handle =|= for multi-arity
5. Arrow =->=  separates argument types from return type
6. Grouped types =[...]=  for nested function types

- Environment Changes

1. Add =spec-env= or extend existing env with spec bindings
2. Key: name, Value: (docstring, list of type signatures)

- Type Checker Changes

1. On =defn=, lookup spec for name
2. If found, check implementation against spec
3. If not found, proceed with inference or require inline types

---

- Future Extensions (Not for This PR)

#+begin_src prologos
spec add Nat Nat -> Nat
  :examples
    [add 0 0] => 0
    [add 1 2] => 3
  :properties
    commutative : [add x y] = [add y x]
    associative : [add [add x y] z] = [add x [add y z]]
#+end_src

These will require:
- Keyword parsing (=:examples=, =:properties=)
- Property/example AST nodes
- Integration with test framework

---

- Test Cases

#+begin_src prologos
;; Test 1: Simple spec + defn
spec inc Nat -> Nat
defn inc [x] [+ x 1]
;; Should pass type check

;; Test 2: Polymorphic
spec id A -> A
defn id [x] x
;; Should pass, A unified with any type

;; Test 3: Multi-arity
spec greet
  | String -> String
  | String String -> String
defn greet
  | [name] [concat "Hello, " name]
  | [greeting name] [concat greeting name]
;; Should pass

;; Test 4: Dependent
spec head (xs : Vec [suc n] A) -> A
defn head [xs]
  [vec/first xs]
;; Should pass, non-empty vector guaranteed

;; Test 5: Mismatch (should error)
spec double Nat -> Nat
defn double [x] [concat x x]
;; Should fail: String vs Nat

;; Test 6: Missing defn (should warn)
spec orphan Nat -> Bool
;; Warning: no implementation

;; Test 7: Missing spec (should infer or require inline)
defn mystery [x y] [+ x y]
;; Should infer: Nat Nat -> Nat (or require inline types)
#+end_src

---

This spec form lays the groundwork for our generative testing and property discovery features. Once keywords are implemented, we can extend =spec= with =:examples=, =:properties=, and =:derive=.



* Let's try to define a number of point free functions with Prologos

- Point-Free Programming in Prologos

Let's explore point-free style using =spec= and our syntax.

---

- Basic Combinators

#+begin_src prologos
spec id
  "Identity function."
  A -> A
defn id [x] x

spec const
  "Constant function. Returns first argument, ignores second."
  A B -> A
defn const [x y] x

spec flip
  "Flip argument order."
  [A B -> C] -> [B A -> C]
defn flip [f]
  [fn [x y] [f y x]]

spec compose
  "Compose two functions. (g ∘ f)(x) = g(f(x))"
  [B -> C] [A -> B] -> [A -> C]
defn compose [g f]
  [fn [x] [g [f x]]]

spec pipe
  "Reverse composition. (f |> g)(x) = g(f(x))"
  [A -> B] [B -> C] -> [A -> C]
defn pipe [f g]
  [compose g f]

spec apply
  "Apply function to argument."
  [A -> B] A -> B
defn apply [f x]
  [f x]
#+end_src

---

- Composition Operators

#+begin_src prologos
spec >>
  "Forward pipe operator."
  [A -> B] [B -> C] -> [A -> C]
defn >> [f g]
  [fn [x] [g [f x]]]

spec <<
  "Backward pipe operator."
  [B -> C] [A -> B] -> [A -> C]
defn << [g f]
  [fn [x] [g [f x]]]

spec >>>
  "Triple compose forward."
  [A -> B] [B -> C] [C -> D] -> [A -> D]
defn >>> [f g h]
  [>> [>> f g] h]
#+end_src

---

- Currying and Uncurrying

#+begin_src prologos
spec curry
  "Convert uncurried function to curried."
  [[A B] -> C] -> [A -> [B -> C]]
defn curry [f]
  [fn [a] [fn [b] [f [pair a b]]]]

spec uncurry
  "Convert curried function to uncurried."
  [A -> [B -> C]] -> [[A B] -> C]
defn uncurry [f]
  [fn [p] [[f [fst p]] [snd p]]]

spec curry3
  "Curry a 3-argument function."
  [[A B C] -> D] -> [A -> [B -> [C -> D]]]
defn curry3 [f]
  [fn [a] [fn [b] [fn [c] [f [triple a b c]]]]]
#+end_src

---

- Functor / Applicative / Monad Combinators

#+begin_src prologos
spec fmap
  "Map over a functor."
  [A -> B] [F A] -> F B
defn fmap [f fa]
  [map f fa]

spec <$>
  "Infix fmap."
  [A -> B] [F A] -> F B
defn <$> [f fa]
  [fmap f fa]

spec pure
  "Lift value into applicative."
  A -> F A
defn pure [x]
  [wrap x]

spec <*>
  "Applicative apply."
  [F [A -> B]] [F A] -> F B
defn <*> [ff fa]
  [ap ff fa]

spec >>=
  "Monadic bind."
  [M A] [A -> M B] -> M B
defn >>= [ma f]
  [bind ma f]

spec >=
  "Kleisli composition."
  [A -> M B] [B -> M C] -> [A -> M C]
defn >= [f g]
  [fn [a] [>>= [f a] g]]

spec join
  "Flatten nested monad."
  M [M A] -> M A
defn join [mma]
  [>>= mma id]
#+end_src

---

- Arrow Combinators

#+begin_src prologos
spec first
  "Apply function to first element of pair."
  [A -> B] -> [[A C] -> [B C]]
defn first [f]
  [fn [p] [pair [f [fst p]] [snd p]]]

spec second
  "Apply function to second element of pair."
  [B -> C] -> [[A B] -> [A C]]
defn second [g]
  [fn [p] [pair [fst p] [g [snd p]]]]

spec ***
  "Apply two functions to pair elements."
  [A -> B] [C -> D] -> [[A C] -> [B D]]
defn *** [f g]
  [fn [p] [pair [f [fst p]] [g [snd p]]]]

spec &&&
  "Fan out: apply both functions to same input."
  [A -> B] [A -> C] -> [A -> [B C]]
defn &&& [f g]
